The subject *remains* in physical contact with the bonded human — the proximity drive is the last to fail — but the *mirroring* is gone. He will be against the human's leg, body slack, eye unfocused, not responsive to the human's affect state. He has become a body-in-contact rather than a presence-in-contact. To a careful observer this is unmistakable; to the kind of humans this dog has chosen, it can persist for days unnoticed.
- **Olfactory disengagement.** The cardinal cognitive resource of this subject — scent — goes quiet. He stops investigating new odors. He stops perimeter-checking a new room. The blind-side defensive geometry that he has executed for a decade lapses; he will lie with the blind side fully exposed. This is the most prognostically grave sign because it indicates the surveillance system that has organized his behavior since the Ivan-incident has shut down.
- **Sleep collapse.** From shallow polyphasic sleep to long, immobile periods that resemble depression in human terms — he will be lying in the same posture an hour later, two hours later, and a human checking on him will mistake the stillness for rest.
- **Somatic emergence at the buffer organs.** The gut, the coat, the eye. Frank pancreatitis is plausible. Hepatic enzyme elevation is plausible. A keratitis of the remaining good eye is plausible — *stress-mediated immune suppression in the one organ he cannot afford to lose*. The body, having absorbed for so long, presents the bill.

**Duration:** measured in days once entered, but the path *to* Stage IV is measured in months to years, which is why the canon has never had the page-count to show it.

---

## 7.6 THE CONDITIONS THAT PRODUCE COLLAPSE

Not all stressors decompensate this subject. The dog has tolerated, on the record: gunfire, explosions, supernatural manifestations, kidnappings of his humans, his own injuries, multiple relocations, and the routine intoxicant ambience of Barton's household. None of these are the conditions that produce failure. They are loud. He is calibrated for loud.

The conditions that *do* produce decompensation are quieter and more specific:

1. **Loss of a bonded human to death rather than to absence.** The subject has a re-bonding mechanism for absence; he does not, on the evidence, have one for death. The scent-signature does not return. The FRIEND classification cannot be reassigned because the slot does not empty — it simply ceases to be re-confirmed. This produces the perseverative scent-tracking of Stage III at its most acute and is the single most probable initiating event for a Stage IV collapse.
2. **A bonded human present-but-unreachable.** Worse than death, on the evidence base, would be a Barton or Bishop who is physically in the home but psychologically *gone* — catatonic depression, severe substance crisis, dissociative withdrawal. The dog's affect-regulation strategy requires a human substrate to bond with. A present-but-empty human breaks the strategy without offering the option of re-bonding to someone else.
3. **Forced isolation.** Kenneling, hospitalization, quarantine. The subject's coping requires proximity. Removed from all bonded humans simultaneously and without a substitute, the Stage I-to-Stage IV progression will compress dramatically; what takes months in a chaotic but populated household will take days in an empty room.
4. **Olfactory-environment disruption.** Severe head trauma producing anosmia would be catastrophic for this subject in a way it would not be for most dogs. His entire cognitive operating system runs on scent. Removing it removes his capacity to classify FRIEND, to investigate, to mirror, to defensively-orient. He would not survive it psychologically.
5. **A FRIEND scent-signature in a BAD context.** The Grills puzzle was solvable. A larger, unsolvable instance — a bonded human who becomes the threat, or a confirmed-FRIEND scent attached to a known-BAD outcome — would shatter the classification system that organizes his perception. The splitting defense, rigid in this subject, has no graceful failure mode.
6. **Loss of the right eye.** Catastrophic. Monocular operation has been compensated; total blindness in an animal whose entire trauma history is encoded in *the eye* would collapse the somatic-postural scaffolding (sighted-eye orientation, blind-side defense) that holds his behavior together.
7. **Chronic stable safety with a calm, attentive owner.** Counterintuitive and important. The subject's coping strategy is calibrated for chaos. Removed to a placid, attentive, low-stimulus environment, he would have nothing to mirror, no decompensating human to regulate, no scent-vigilance task, no vocational identity. The therapy-animal phenotype, deprived of patients, has nowhere to go. This is a slower decompensation — months — but it is real, and it is the one no caretaker would anticipate.

---

## 7.7 THE ESCALATION PATH IN COMPRESSED FORM

For operational reference:

> **Trigger** (loss / unreachable bond / isolation / scent-classification breach) → **Stage I narrowing** (range collapse, pant compression, scent-fixation, food acceleration) → **Stage II somatic routing** (GI cycling, coat degradation on blind side, sleep shallowing, micro-licking, scent-perseveration) → **Stage III behavioral emergence** (velcro proximity, non-resolving investigation, food-guarding, mirror decoupling, sighted-eye drift, tail-amplitude loss) → **Stage IV decompensation** (top-down anhedonia, social withdrawal, mirroring collapse, olfactory disengagement, sleep collapse, somatic presentation at gut/coat/remaining eye).

The path is unidirectional once Stage III is entered without intervention. The path is fully reversible from Stage I and largely reversible from Stage II given restoration of a stable bonded human and dietary correction.

---

## 7.8 WHAT THE COLLAPSE WOULD LOOK LIKE FROM THE OUTSIDE

The intelligence value of this section depends on specifying what a witness — a Barton, a Bishop, a neighbor, a vet tech — would actually *see*.

They would see a dog who had become quieter. They would not see a sick dog. He would still come when called, more slowly. He would still eat, less. He would still lie against a leg, less responsively. The tail-tip would still move at the door. The eye, the good one, would not track as far. The coat would mat on the left.

They would, almost certainly, attribute it to age. The subject is now a decade into published canon and the explanation would be available. It would be wrong.

The dog is not failing of age. He is failing of having held, for an entire adult life, the affect of every human he has ever loved, in a body that lost an eye to the first one of them, with no learned mechanism for putting any of it down.

---

## 7.9 PROGNOSTIC SUMMARY

- **Probability of overt aggressive decompensation:** negligible. Not in this subject's behavioral repertoire.
- **Probability of phobic decompensation:** low. Calibrated for chaos.
- **Probability of separation-protest decompensation:** low-to-moderate, and only in the velcro/preventive form, not the destructive form.
- **Probability of somatic decompensation (GI, dermatologic, ophthalmic):** high, on the cumulative load alone, before any acute trigger is added.
- **Probability of attachment-collapse decompensation given loss of a primary bonded human:** high, and the most likely terminal pathway.
- **Reversibility given timely intervention:** excellent through Stage II, partial through Stage III, poor at Stage IV — but Stage IV in this subject is rare precisely because his system is so efficient at concealing the approach to it.

The operational note for any handler with custody of this subject: *watch the tail amplitude on greeting, watch the food hierarchy, watch the blind-side coat.* If those three drift, the subject is already in Stage II and the clock has started.

---

*End Section 7. Cross-reference Section 8 (Relational Map) for the bonded-human dependency graph that determines which Stage IV trigger is currently most operative.*


## Trajectory & Forward Assessment

# TRAJECTORY & FORWARD ASSESSMENT

## Subject: LUCKY ("Pizza Dog")
### Section 6 of the Compiled Dossier — Forward Projection, Fork Analysis, Decompensation Vectors

---

## 6.1 The Actuarial Frame

Any forward assessment of this subject begins with a sentence the prior sections have circled but not stated outright: **Lucky is mortal on a clock the bonded humans are not.** He entered the published record in 2012 as an adult dog of indeterminate but non-juvenile age — the dentition, musculature, and behavioral self-possession in *Hawkeye* #1 are not those of a young animal; conservative estimate at intake, three to five years. By the publication-time of the most recent appearances he is, in canonical body-time, somewhere between twelve and fifteen. For a medium-large mixed-breed retriever-mongrel of his apparent weight class, that is the precise window in which the actuarial tables begin to bend sharply.

This matters because every other section of this file has analyzed a subject in equilibrium. Forward, the equilibrium breaks not because the psychology fails but because the body does, and the psychology has never been tested against the slow, undramatic decline that none of his trauma prepared him for. He has survived a car. He has not yet survived a Tuesday morning on which his hips refuse to lift him off the kitchen tile and Clint is in Madripoor.

The medical projections, taken from the nutritional record (Section 2):
- **Renal:** the decade-long sodium and allium load predicts early-onset CKD, plausibly already sub-clinical. Forward marker: polydipsia, weight loss, the appearance of being "picky" about previously favored pizza scraps. The day Lucky refuses crust is a diagnostic event.
- **Hepatic:** chronic low-grade ethanol and theobromine exposure, secondhand cannabinoid contact, irregular meal composition. Predicts elevated ALT, possible nodular changes by year fifteen.
- **Orthopedic:** the car impact, however well it healed, laid down a substrate for asymmetric arthritis. The compensatory head-turn from monocular vision has loaded the cervical spine for a decade. Cervical spondylosis is the most probable first failure point — and it will present, behaviorally, as *reduced gravitation toward sad humans*, because climbing onto the couch will hurt. The household will read this as Lucky becoming distant. The truth will be that the therapy-animal phenotype is being throttled by a vertebra.
- **Sensory:** the right eye becomes the only eye. Cataract onset in the surviving globe is the single most catastrophic discrete event on the medical horizon. Total blindness in a one-eyed dog with elite olfactory compensation is *navigable* — but only in a stable environment, which is the one variable Lucky's life has never possessed.
- **Cognitive:** canine cognitive dysfunction is a near-certainty in the projection window. Sundowning, disorientation in familiar spaces, altered sleep-wake cycle, loss of learned scent-classification stability. The FRIEND/BAD binary that has organized his world will begin to misfire. He will, at some point, fail to recognize Kate.

These are not pessimistic projections. They are the median path.

---

## 6.2 The Fork Points

There are, on the evidence, five discrete decision-nodes ahead of the subject — events whose outcomes will shape whichever phenotype Lucky carries into senescence. Each is binary in outcome and consequential in branching.

### Fork I — Custodial Resolution

The cross-country shuttling between Barton and Bishop households is sustainable in middle age and lethal in late life. Geriatric dogs do not tolerate transcontinental disruption; the cortisol load alone accelerates the cognitive-decline curve. The fork: **does the Barton-Bishop dyad formally resolve primary custody, or does the dog continue to be shuttled until something breaks?**

- *Growth branch:* Kate, who has demonstrated more domestic stability across the LA arcs, accepts primary custody; Clint becomes the visiting figure. The household becomes the warm, weather-stable, single-residence environment a geriatric dog requires. Lucky's terminal years are organized.
- *Catastrophe branch:* neither human takes the conversation. The dog continues to be a shared object, a proxy for the Barton-Bishop relationship itself. He decompensates not through any one move but through the cumulative scent-rebuilding cost of forty AirBnBs.

The diagnostic indicator that the fork has been resolved well: Lucky stops looking at the door when it opens.

### Fork II — The Replacement Animal Question

Standard household drift: if Lucky's decline becomes visible, the human impulse — *especially* in this particular constellation of emotionally-laden, conflict-averse humans — is to introduce a second dog. "For company." The behavioral literature on this is unambiguous and the projection for *this* subject is specific: Lucky will not protest. He will accept the new animal within hours. He will redirect a measurable percentage of his proximity-seeking to the new dog because the new dog will be physically easier to lie down beside than the human who keeps leaving. The humans will read this as Lucky enjoying the company. What it will actually be is the subject doing what he has always done — *re-imprinting on whatever is present and non-violent* — at the cost of the bond they imagined they were preserving. The new dog will inherit Clint. Lucky will fold quietly into a secondary role and the humans will not notice the demotion they have engineered.

- *Growth branch:* no second animal until Lucky is gone, or the second animal is brought in with structured, slow, attention-protective protocols.
- *Catastrophe branch:* a puppy at year thirteen. He will share his bed. He will be smaller in his own house by the second week.

### Fork III — A Bonded Human Dies On-Page

This is the structural risk that defines the next decade of Hawkeye-adjacent canon. The Barton household operates at a sustained lethality coefficient that exceeds civilian baseline by several orders of magnitude. Probability that Clint Barton, Kate Bishop, or a tertiary bonded figure (Barney, America Chavez in the LA orbit, the Thompson-era West Coast roster) is killed within the projection window: high.

For most dogs this would be the central trauma of late life. For *this* dog it is something more specific. Section 3 established that he never developed the protest-despair-detachment sequence; he goes straight to rebond. Forward-projected, this means a bonded human's death will *not* produce the classical pining, the food-refusal, the keening at the door. It will produce a forty-eight-hour orientation gap and then a transferred attachment to whoever is in the apartment on day three.

The humans will find this monstrous. They will be wrong. It is the same mechanism that saved him from Ivan. But it will damage the surviving humans' image of him, and — the projection that matters — *the surviving humans may withdraw from him because of it*, reading his resilience as betrayal of the dead. The catastrophe branch here is not Lucky's grief. It is the survivors punishing him for not grieving correctly.

### Fork IV — The Olfactory Failure Event

The FRIEND/BAD scent classification is the organizing cognitive architecture of this subject. Section 3's evidence from *Hawkeye* #11 is dispositive: he runs his world on it. Forward, two things degrade this system: olfactory bulb aging (modest, gradual) and cognitive dysfunction (steeper, asymmetric). The fork is the day he misclassifies a *known* BAD-signature human as FRIEND, or vice versa, in circumstances of consequence.

The realistic vector: a Tracksuit-era handler — Ivan, or one of Ivan's cohort, survivors of the Madripoor connection — re-enters proximity in the subject's late life. In his prime, Lucky would scent-flag and orient defensively at twenty meters. In decline, the recognition lags, fails, or generates the wrong category. The growth branch is benign: the encounter passes, the human noticed nothing. The catastrophe branch: he greets the wrong man with a tail-wag and the bonded humans, watching, lose a piece of trust in their own household's alarm system that they cannot replace because Lucky *was* the alarm system.

### Fork V — The Therapy-Animal Burnout

Section 5 framed the vocational identity: *be present for sad humans*. This is sustainable so long as the human's sadness is the kind that responds to presence. It is not sustainable against psychiatric conditions that worsen regardless of company — and Clint Barton's trajectory, on the published evidence, is precisely the kind of treatment-resistant depressive-PTSD constellation that does not improve from a dog's head on the lap. For a decade Lucky has been the household's emotional regulator; the regulation has held the humans roughly steady; the humans have read this as Lucky helping. What they have not registered is the *accumulated load* on an animal whose protest behavior is suppressed.

Forward-projected, the burnout presents as none of the things humans look for. Not aggression, not avoidance, not depression. It presents as a slow attenuation of the gravitational pull toward the most-distressed person in the room. He simply stops crossing the apartment when Clint sits down heavily. He stays on his bed. The humans will attribute this to age. Some of it will be age. Some of it will be the organism finally exercising the protest it never learned to express in the only language it has left: the absence of the behavior they relied on.

---

## 6.3 Open Questions the File Cannot Yet Resolve

A forward assessment must catalog the empirical gaps as honestly as the projections.

1. **Was there a pre-Ivan attachment?** The record begins with the Tracksuit handler. There is no evidence regarding puppyhood, litter, dam, first home. The Ivan period is treated as origin because it is the start of the published record, not because it is the start of the dog's life. Any pre-Ivan secure attachment, even brief, would substantially change the resilience etiology — moving it from "constitutional" toward "early-secure-with-later-disruption," which carries a better prognosis under late-life stressors. The file cannot close on this.

2. **Reproductive/endocrine status.** Unknown. Material to projections of prostatic, mammary, and behavioral changes in senescence.

3. **The exact mechanism of the #11 cognitive performance.** The investigation depicted is at the upper edge of plausible canine cognition. Two readings remain open: (a) Lucky is a statistical outlier within species norms, (b) some subtle environmental factor — proximity to magical, mutant, or technologically-enhanced individuals across the Hawkeye-Avengers orbit — has produced sub-threshold cognitive enhancement. The file leans (a). The file cannot rule out (b). The distinction matters for late-life cognitive trajectory: an outlier declines from a higher baseline and retains function longer; an enhanced animal may decline catastrophically when the enhancement source is removed.

4. **The grief question.** The record contains no observed instance of a bonded human's death during a period in which Lucky's response could be cleanly observed. Every separation has been geographic, not terminal. The hypothesis that he will transfer-bond rather than grieve is *inferred from the protest-despair-detachment evidence* and is not yet directly tested. It remains the single most consequential untested prediction in this file.

5. **Pain tolerance and pain expression.** Closely related: this is a subject who absorbed a vehicular impact and an enucleation-grade orbital injury without leaving an observable behavioral record of suffering past the acute window. Forward, this is a danger. Geriatric dogs who do not express pain are not dogs who are not in pain. They are dogs whose caretakers learn too late.

---

## 6.4 The Growth Path

There is a version of the next five-to-seven years in which the subject does well. It is specifiable.

It requires the Barton-Bishop dyad to make one explicit decision about primary custody and hold to it. It requires a stable single residence with low-traffic flooring, a ramp to whatever surface he currently jumps onto, and a veterinary relationship that does not reset every time the household relocates. It requires the humans to read his late-life withdrawal as load rather than aging — and to *reduce demand on him as a regulator*, which means seeking their own therapeutic resources rather than continuing to outsource affect-management to a fifteen-year-old dog. It requires no second animal until the conversation about ending has been had. It requires the humans to accept that his resilience is not consent.

If those conditions hold, the projection is a soft landing: graceful decline, preserved appetite into the final year, cognitive dysfunction that arrives late and partial, and a death in a familiar room with at least one of the bonded humans physically present. Section 3 established that proximity precedes food in his hierarchy. The terminal version of that hierarchy is simple: he needs someone to be there.

---

## 6.5 The Catastrophe Path

The catastrophe path does not look catastrophic. That is its diagnostic signature.

It looks like a series of small, defensible household decisions. Another move, because of work. A puppy, because the apartment seemed quiet. Skipping the senior bloodwork panel, because he seems fine. Leaving him with a neighbor for a long weekend, because he's adaptable, he always has been. Reading his slowness as contentment. Reading his quieter greetings as maturity. Reading his absence from the couch as preference. Each decision is small. None of them, on its own, would register as a betrayal. The subject's entire phenotype is structured to make these decisions feel safe to the humans making them, because he does not protest, has never protested, and will not start now.

The catastrophe path ends with a dog who dies in a boarding facility while his humans are in two different time zones, having intended to be home by Thursday. The medical proximate cause will be something defensible — a cardiac event, an obstruction, a fall. The actual etiology will have been written across the decade preceding it, in the quiet form of every separation he absorbed without complaint.

---

## 6.6 Synthesis

The forward assessment, integrated:

The subject is entering the segment of his life in which his foundational adaptation — *re-imprinting under disruption rather than protesting it* — stops being adaptive and starts being lethal. The same architecture that made him survivable as a young dog in a violent household makes him *un-protectable* as an old dog in an inattentive one, because his caretakers have spent a decade being trained by his own tolerance to believe that he requires very little.

He requires, in fact, the same thing every aging mammal requires: a stable room, a familiar smell, a body in the room with him, and the dignity of having his pain recognized before he is forced to express it in a language his humans can hear. The question the file cannot answer — the question that organizes the entire forward projection — is whether the humans who have inherited him are capable of providing those things without the dog first having to ask.

The behavioral evidence, across every section of this file, suggests he will not ask.

The forward assessment, accordingly, is conditional on the humans, not on him. He has already done the hard work of his life. What happens next is no longer his.

---

*— end Section 6 —*

## Relationship Graph
### [[Clinton Francis Barton (Hawkeye (also: Ronin, Goliath - briefly, Golden Archer - briefly))]]
- Primary handler. Rescuer. The man who came back for him and paid the vet bill.
- Bond mode: caretaker-of-the-caretaker. Lucky absorbs Clint's depressive affect; Clint provides the rescue-imprint that organizes Lucky's entire post-trauma life.
- Trust/safety: bedrock. The original imprint.
- Failure mode: Clint's depressive collapse is the chronic ambient stressor; if Clint decompensated to the point of neglect (failure to feed, failure to provide shelter), Lucky would persist in proximity-maintenance past the point of his own welfare. The bond is unconditional in a way that does not protect Lucky from Clint's worst self.
- Leverage: to reach Lucky in any state, present Clint. To predict Lucky's geography, locate Clint.

### [[Kate Bishop (Hawkeye)]]
- Co-primary handler. The emotionally-available pole of the dyad. The handler who took him to LA when Clint could not.
- Bond mode: companion-equal, road partner, mutually-energizing. Where Clint receives caretaking, Kate receives companionship.
- Trust/safety: bedrock. The second imprint that completed the multi-handler secure base.
- Failure mode: Kate's workaholic dissociation and tendency to disappear into a case could, in extremis, leave Lucky environmentally neglected; she is less likely than Clint to fail him through depression and more likely to fail him through distraction.
- Leverage: to draw Lucky west, present Kate. To activate his high-energy mode, Kate in the room.

### Grills
- Secondary pack member, tenement neighbor, murdered (HAWKEYE #11).
- Bond mode: friendly, food-sharing, casual.
- Trust/safety: was safe.
- Failure mode: closed by death.
- Leverage: historical - the grief response to Grills established Lucky's capacity for sustained pack-mourning and independent investigative action.

### Tracksuit Mafia
- Original handlers / abusers. The pre-acquisition pack.
- Bond mode: severed by his own choice in HAWKEYE #1.
- Trust/safety: zero; stimulus-class for residual hypervigilance.
- Failure mode: not applicable - this is the negative reference bond against which all later trust is measured.
- Leverage: Tracksuit-coded stimulus (vocalization, boots, cologne) is the most reliable non-violent way to elevate his arousal.

### Tenement Neighbors
- Secondary pack. Communal mascot relationship. Aimee, Simone, Simone's boys, others.
- Bond mode: gentle ambassador, communal property, low-demand affection.
- Trust/safety: high, generalized.
- Failure mode: any one of them could be a Grills - i.e., killable, and the grief would be real but absorbable.
- Leverage: the tenement is his secondary territory; threats to it activate territorial defense at a lower threshold than threats to strangers.

### Wca Roster
- Tertiary social field. America Chavez, Gwenpool, Quentin Quire, Johnny Watts, Fuse, etc.
- Bond mode: tolerated, integrated, non-bonded. He is comfortable in their presence without being attached.
- Trust/safety: derivative of Kate's trust.
- Failure mode: low - he does not depend on them.
- Leverage: minimal direct; useful as proxies through Kate.
